CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0004♔CMS-2025-0050♔David Thomas♔Coalition for Health AI (CHAI)♔♔♔♔The Coalition for Health AI opposes removing AI model card transparency requirements for healthcare systems, arguing that the absence of outcome studies demonstrating their value should trigger regulatory caution rather than deregulation, particul...♔Re: Request for Information Related to the Health Technology Ecosystem – Deregulatory Actions to Unleash Prosperity<br><br>The Coalition for Health AI (CHAI) appreciates the opportunity to comment on the proposed deregulatory framework. We represent stakeholders across healthcare institutions, AI developers, and patient advocates who share a commitment to innovation. However, we have significant concerns about removing AI model card transparency requirements during a period when healthcare AI adoption is accelerating faster than our ability to study its effects.<br><br>Last spring, my sister was diagnosed with a rare autoimmune condition. We spent months moving her records between specialists, and what struck me wasn't just the inconvenience—it was that no one could tell us definitively what data existed, where it lived, or who was using it for what purpose. A billing company called about a test she'd already paid for. That experience taught me something: access without accountability creates a different kind of problem. We fixed the portability issue, but we created new questions about whether those moved records were being used safely, especially when algorithms started making decisions about what got flagged as "relevant" for the next provider.<br><br>The agency's analysis suggests that absent published outcome studies demonstrating AI model card transparency benefits, these requirements lack demonstrated value and should be removed. We respectfully disagree with this framing. The absence of robust longitudinal evidence does not establish absence of benefit; it reflects that detailed transparency requirements are too recent to have generated the studies themselves. This inverts the burden of proof in healthcare contexts where trust is foundational.<br><br>Consider the timeline: significant healthcare AI deployment is occurring now. Transparency requirements are nascent. We cannot wait for a decade of outcome studies before building safeguards into systems already making clinical decisions. The evidence vacuum should trigger caution, not deregulation by default.<br><br>We urge ONC to maintain detailed AI model card disclosure requirements, particularly for systems affecting clinical decision-making or patient safety. These transparency standards are preconditions for trust, not luxuries awaiting empirical vindication. We're happy to discuss implementation approaches that balance burden reduction with meaningful oversight.<br><br>David Thomas<br>Policy Director, Coalition for Health AI<br>North Dakota♔♔2026-01-15T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-15T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔1♔♔♔♔TRUE♔1♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔The agency's claim that no public evidence supports transparency's positive impact conflates lack of published studies with lack of real-world benefit; transparency requirements are too recent and nascent to have generated robust outcome literature.♔advocacy_group♔medium♔concerned♔North Dakota♔policy director♔54♔Last spring, my sister was diagnosed with a rare autoimmune condition, and we spent months shuttling between specialists trying to piece together her medical history from different clinics—one hospital's records system wouldn't talk to another's. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0005♔CMS-2025-0050♔Amanda Perez♔The American College of Obstetricians and Gynecologists♔♔♔♔Removing model card and disclosure requirements will worsen healthcare fragmentation by leaving hospitals unable to transparently evaluate and safely integrate AI tools across institutional boundaries; instead, regulators should maintain baseline ...♔Last year, my research team at the College tried to do something straightforward: integrate electronic health records across three hospital systems in Western Pennsylvania to track pregnancy outcomes and maternal safety trends. We couldn't do it. The systems were certified under different standards, and each hospital's legal and compliance teams were terrified of the liability exposure if they tried to force interoperability without explicit certification alignment. A pregnant patient I'm still thinking about slipped through the cracks because her prenatal labs from the first hospital never reached her OB at the second one. That's when it became clear to me that the fragmentation we're trying to solve isn't really about certification burden—it's about the fact that hospitals have no trustworthy, standardized way to understand what they're actually adopting when they buy a new AI tool or health IT system.<br><br>I want to be direct about what concerns me in this deregulation proposal. Removing model card requirements and disclosure standards will make that problem worse, not better. When procurement teams at hospital systems evaluate AI tools for clinical deployment—especially tools that cross institutional boundaries like maternal health risk stratification or diagnostic decision support—they need documented, standardized information about what the model does, how it was trained, and what populations it's been tested on. That's not bureaucratic overhead; that's decision-making infrastructure. Right now, hospitals can't confidently say yes to interoperability because they can't compare tools transparently. Remove the model card requirement and you don't reduce burden; you shift it entirely onto each hospital to independently reverse-engineer what they're buying.<br><br>The tension everyone's discussing is real: we do need to reduce unnecessary burden on developers. But the answer isn't weaker transparency standards. It's smarter certification criteria that require model cards and basic performance documentation while streamlining redundant institutional review. I'd recommend keeping DSI model card disclosure as a baseline requirement but exempting certain low-risk classes of tools from extended validation timelines. That way, developers get faster market access for tools with transparent documentation, and hospitals can actually integrate systems across care settings because they have the information they need to make it safe. That's how you get both innovation and interoperability.♔♔2025-12-08T00:00:00Z♔2025-12-08T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔1♔♔♔♔TRUE♔2♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔Hospital and health system procurement teams rely on DSI transparency requirements to evaluate whether AI tools are fit for their specific patient populations, clinical workflows, and risk tolerances; removal strips away decision-making data.♔academic♔high♔urgent♔Pennsylvania♔PhD researcher♔32♔When my research team tried to integrate electronic health records across three hospital systems in Western Pennsylvania last year to track pregnancy outcomes, we hit a wall—the systems literally couldn't communicate because they were certified under different standards, and no one wanted to risk the liability of forcing interoperability. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0006♔CMS-2025-0050♔Ashley King♔Verve, LLC♔♔♔♔A mid-sized health IT vendor argues that AI model transparency requirements (including training data, intended use, and performance limitations) must remain rigorous and uniformly applied in certification revisions, as uneven removal of these requ...♔Thank you for the opportunity to comment on the proposed certification revisions.<br><br>Two years ago, our health IT division spent eighteen months and $300,000 to certify a single module. During that same period, a competitor launched functionally identical capability in six weeks. They had the cash to absorb regulatory uncertainty; we didn't. That gap troubles me now as we look at what's being proposed here, because streamlining certification unevenly—removing transparency requirements while keeping others intact—will create exactly the kind of uneven playing field that favors vendors who can weather chaos.<br><br>I want to be direct: I'm skeptical of arguments that removing AI model card disclosure requirements reduces unnecessary burden. What they actually reduce is visibility. When a larger vendor can deploy an AI-assisted diagnostic module without documenting its training data, intended use, or known performance gaps, and a smaller vendor can't, the smaller vendor loses the most important competitive advantage transparency offers—predictability. Right now, because we're mid-sized, we can't absorb the cost of waiting out regulatory rewrites. We need to know the rules and know they apply equally. Detailed model cards level that ground.<br><br>The harder question is what harm prevention looks like. I've watched health IT roll out features that seemed reasonable in the lab and caused real problems at scale—nothing catastrophic, but persistent enough that they lingered for months. With AI, the stakes are different. Waiting for documented patient harm before requiring transparency is ethically indefensible in this space. We can prevent problems by requiring upfront disclosure of what these models were built on, what populations they were validated against, and where they're likely to fail. That's not regulatory burden; it's basic accountability.<br><br>On your second question about maintenance of certification: I'd suggest decoupling AI transparency requirements from general certification lightening. Transparency standards should stay rigorous and uniform precisely because they protect competition. Everything else—the recertification cycles, the attestation documentation, the interoperability testing frequency—those are fair game for streamlining. But if you reduce transparency requirements unevenly or eliminate them while keeping traditional certification criteria, you've created a playing field where deep-pocketed vendors win by default because they can afford regulatory ambiguity and smaller vendors can't.<br><br>The concern I keep circling back to is this: if you make the rules predictable and uniform, everyone can plan. We can staff for it. We can build it into our development timeline. What we can't do—what I literally cannot do with our current resources—is compete in an environment where the rules change unpredictably and only vendors with massive reserves can absorb the cost of that change.<br><br>Please keep AI transparency requirements as part of any certification modernization. Make them non-negotiable and apply them equally. That's the only scenario where smaller vendors aren't squeezed out.<br><br>Respectfully,<br><br>Ashley King<br>Vice President, Regulatory Affairs<br>Verve, LLC<br>Mississippi♔♔2025-12-23T00:00:00Z♔2025-12-23T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔1♔♔♔♔TRUE♔1♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔Few DSIs are currently AI-based, but the criterion must be in place proactively to prevent harm as AI adoption accelerates; waiting for documented failures before requiring transparency is ethically indefensible.♔industry♔medium♔concerned♔Mississippi♔VP of regulatory affairs♔54♔When our smaller health IT division spent eighteen months and nearly $300,000 getting one module certified two years ago, I watched a larger competitor launch virtually identical functionality in six weeks with half the resources—they had the budget to absorb regulatory uncertainty in ways we just don't. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0007♔CMS-2025-0050♔Michelle Martinez♔Dental Standards Institute♔♔♔♔The Dental Standards Institute opposes removing transparency and auditability certification criteria, arguing that the absence of published outcome studies does not justify dismantling safeguards that are operationally necessary for breach detecti...♔Submitted via regulations.gov<br><br>The Dental Standards Institute appreciates the opportunity to comment on the proposed deregulatory actions. Our organization works with dental practices, insurers, and patient advocates across the Pacific Northwest, and we have a direct stake in how transparency and accountability standards evolve in healthcare IT.<br><br>I want to address the RFI's framing around certification criteria and the absence of published outcome studies on model card transparency. This framing concerns me because it conflates two separate claims in a way that doesn't hold up to scrutiny. Yes, there are few peer-reviewed studies measuring the downstream impact of AI model transparency requirements. But the absence of those studies does not mean transparency lacks real-world benefit. These transparency standards are still new—we're talking about requirements that are only a few years old at most. We shouldn't expect a robust literature base to exist yet. The agency seems to be using that gap in evidence to justify removing safeguards that are operationally necessary right now.<br><br>Last year, a regional dental network experienced a significant breach. Patient records, including my daughter's orthodontic history and insurance details, were exposed for nearly two months before anyone detected it. The incident report revealed the practice had deliberately disabled interoperability alerts to streamline workflows. There was no audit trail. No one knew when records were accessed or who had looked at them. The absence of transparency requirements at that practice didn't make operations more efficient in any way that mattered—it just made it impossible to detect what was happening until damage was done.<br><br>This is the operational necessity I'm talking about. Detailed disclosures of model behavior, training data, validation processes—these aren't academic exercises. They are breach detection mechanisms. They create accountability. Real incidents of data mishandling demonstrate that these safeguards prevent harm, even if we haven't published randomized controlled trials proving it yet.<br><br>On the question of which certification criteria should be removed: I would strongly caution against removing transparency and auditability requirements on the assumption that outcome literature doesn't yet exist. That logic inverts what we know. Let the standards stay in place, let them generate real-world experience, and then measure what happens. Don't use the newness of the standards as justification to dismantle them before we have any chance to learn whether they work.♔♔2026-01-09T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-09T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔1♔♔♔♔TRUE♔1♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔The agency's claim that no public evidence supports transparency's positive impact conflates lack of published studies with lack of real-world benefit; transparency requirements are too recent and nascent to have generated robust outcome literature.♔advocacy_group♔high♔urgent♔Oregon♔senior policy analyst♔59♔Last year, our institute received a breach notification from a regional dental network—patient records, including my own daughter's orthodontic history and insurance details, were exposed for nearly two months before anyone noticed. The incident report later revealed the practice had deliberately disabled interoperability alerts to "streamline workflows," leaving no audit trail of when records were accessed or shared. I watched my daughter's anxiety spike as we navigated credit monitoring and notification letters, and I realized we were asking patients to trust a system with almost no real-time visibility into who was handling their most intimate health information.♔The absence of published outcome studies on AI model card transparency does not constitute evidence that transparency lacks real-world benefit; transparency requirements are too recent and nascent to have generated robust outcome literature, and the agency's conflation of these two distinct claims undermines sound policy reasoning. | Real-world incidents of data mishandling, audit trail failures, and delayed breach detection demonstrate that transparency and accountability mechanisms—including detailed disclosures of model behavior, training data, and validation processes—are operationally necessary for patient safety, even absent formal peer-reviewed studies.♔A policy analyst grounded in patient advocacy argues that the absence of published evidence should not be weaponized to justify removing transparency safeguards that are foundational to accountability and breach detection in high-stakes healthcare AI, especially when such safeguards are too new to have generated robust outcome literature.♔♔True♔♔379
CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0008♔CMS-2025-0050♔Kimberly Hill♔Alliance for Connected Care♔♔♔♔The commenter urges retention of AI model card disclosure requirements in health IT certification, arguing that transparency about algorithmic limitations and training data is essential for clinicians to safely evaluate whether systems are appropr...♔My mother's oncology team in Des Moines spent three weeks trying to obtain her pathology slides from an imaging center forty miles away. Those three weeks mattered. While we waited for copies on a USB drive, she waited to start treatment, and I watched my family navigate a healthcare system that couldn't seem to talk to itself even when lives were on the line. That experience is what brought me to this work, and it's why I'm deeply concerned about the current proposal to weaken AI transparency requirements in health IT certification.<br><br>The Alliance for Connected Care supports removing barriers to data sharing. We really do. Information blocking harms patients in concrete ways—we see it constantly in our member organizations. But I've also learned something harder in the past year: faster access to data without rigorous safeguards isn't automatically better. My mother's medical records were finally shared, yes, but I never learned what security measures were actually in place as those files moved between systems. I still don't know who had access to them, or whether the pathology lab and the oncology clinic had any real agreement about how to handle that information. Speed matters, but so does the ability to know what you're getting into when you share something as sensitive as someone's cancer diagnosis.<br><br>I'm writing to urge the Office of the National Coordinator to retain the current certification requirement that health IT developers disclose detailed AI model cards as part of the data sharing infrastructure (DSI) criterion. This isn't about slowing innovation or protecting legacy systems. It's about maintaining a minimal baseline of transparency in software that will make life-or-death decisions about which patients get which treatments.<br><br>Let me explain what I mean by stepping back to the problem itself. The proposal assumes that reducing compliance burden on developers will naturally increase competition, lower costs, and improve data sharing. That logic makes sense in many markets. But health IT isn't a normal market, and AI in clinical settings isn't a normal technology. When a developer builds an algorithm to identify which patients should be prioritized for a particular treatment pathway, or to flag which records are most relevant to a clinical question, those decisions embed assumptions about whose data was used to train the system, what outcomes it was optimized for, and what populations it was tested on. Those assumptions have real consequences for real patients.<br><br>My concern isn't theoretical. I've been reading through the clinical literature on algorithmic bias in healthcare, and the pattern is consistent: systems trained primarily on data from academic medical centers perform worse for rural patients. Systems optimized for one demographic group show higher error rates for others. A model that works reasonably well at Mayo Clinic might systematically misclassify conditions in a smaller hospital in Iowa. None of this means the algorithms are inherently bad—it means they have known limitations and known risks that depend entirely on context. A clinician needs to understand those limitations before relying on the system.<br><br>The current DSI certification requirement for AI model cards asks developers to disclose exactly that information: intended use, target population, known performance gaps, training data characteristics, validation processes, and residual risks. This isn't busywork. This is the minimum documentation needed for a healthcare provider to understand whether a system is actually appropriate for their patient population and their clinical workflows. Without it, we're asking clinicians to adopt software they can't evaluate, and we're asking patients to trust systems nobody is required to explain.<br><br>I recognize the concern about compliance burden. I do. Smaller developers have told us that detailed documentation requirements increase costs and slow time-to-market. That's real. But the solution isn't to eliminate the requirement—it's to get clearer about what's actually necessary and what's just procedural redundancy. The current model card requirement isn't excessive. It's asking for documentation that any competent developer should already have internally. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0011♔CMS-2025-0050♔James Clark♔Federation of State Medical Boards♔♔♔♔The Federation of State Medical Boards opposes weakening transparency and accountability requirements for clinical AI systems, arguing that strengthened model card disclosures and ongoing certification are essential safeguards to prevent algorithm...♔To the Office of the National Coordinator for Health Information Technology and the Assistant Secretary for Technology Policy:<br><br>The Federation of State Medical Boards submits this comment on the proposed deregulatory framework for health IT certification and interoperability standards. A contractor's system had been breached. Nobody discovered it for three years. When she finally got answers, the clinic's response was that their security procedures "met standards at the time." She had to hire a lawyer to find out who accessed what. This is not a theoretical privacy concern. It is what happens when we prioritize streamlined access over the actual infrastructure that prevents misuse.<br><br>On the question of which certification criteria should be removed or revised: we believe model card disclosures should be strengthened, not eliminated or weakened. Model cards are not bureaucratic overhead. They are the minimum transparency standard needed to prevent silent system failures and algorithmic harm. When a clinical AI system makes decisions about patient care—medication recommendations, diagnostic support, treatment planning—someone needs to be able to audit what that system is actually doing. Without detailed model documentation, there is no way to detect whether an algorithm is being applied appropriately, whether it is perpetuating bias, or whether it has been compromised. This is not innovation theater. It is accountability.<br><br>The "black box" problem in healthcare AI directly enables the kind of unauthorized access and unaccountable data use my daughter experienced. If regulators and clinicians cannot see how algorithmic systems process patient information, they cannot audit for misuse. They cannot trace what went wrong after something breaks. Patients have no recourse. Removing detailed certification criteria would repeat the pattern of "standards at the time" excuses that leave patients defenseless.<br><br>We also urge caution regarding proposals to reduce conditions and maintenance of certification requirements. I understand the appeal of this framing. Compliance costs money. But in healthcare, the cost of weak standards is borne by patients, not by developers. We need stronger safeguards for clinical AI, not weaker ones. Model cards, transparency requirements, and ongoing certification are the floor. That's the frustrating part: the better solution lost out not because it was inferior, but because compliance barriers priced out innovation.<br><br>Now I'm seeing the proposed relief, and honestly, it worries me. They have legal teams, regulatory experts, the institutional knowledge to navigate whatever remains. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0016♔CMS-2025-0050♔Barbara Scott♔beHuman Corp.♔♔♔♔BeHuman Corp. argues that comprehensive AI model card disclosure requirements for health IT certification should be retained because they ensure vendor transparency about algorithm limitations and performance gaps, enabling healthcare systems to m...♔Thank you for the opportunity to comment on the proposed modifications to health IT certification requirements. We did this because we believed that standard was necessary, and frankly, because we had no choice if we wanted our product to be trusted by the systems we were trying to integrate with. Now, as we hear that the agency is considering streamlined approval pathways that would allow competitors to bypass substantial portions of these requirements through alternative API standards, I'll be honest: my team and I are concerned we got caught holding an expensive bag while the rules change.<br><br>But that concern, while real and worth acknowledging, isn't actually the core of what we need to say here. What matters more is that the current DSI certification criterion requiring comprehensive AI model card disclosure is doing exactly what it's supposed to do—it's forcing vendors like us to be transparent about what our algorithms actually do, who they work for, and where they might fail. In healthcare, that transparency isn't a competitive burden. It's the foundation for patient safety and clinical trust.<br><br>Let me explain why this matters by walking through what happened in our certification process. Our scheduling algorithm uses machine learning to predict no-show rates based on patient history, appointment type, and demographic factors. On the surface, that sounds straightforward. But the devil is in the details. We had to document that our training data came primarily from three large health systems in the Southeast, which meant our model performed differently for rural populations and smaller practices. We discovered a 12% performance gap for certain age groups. We had to be explicit about that. We had to detail our validation methodology, which included holdout testing across different patient populations and clinic types. We had to identify the failure modes—the scenarios where the algorithm simply doesn't work well—and flag them clearly.<br><br>That process was expensive and time-consuming. Our compliance team spent months working with our data scientists to produce documentation that met the criterion. But here's what actually happened as a result: when we pitched this product to health systems, we weren't asking them to trust our marketing claims. We were handing them a detailed technical document that let their IT directors and clinical leaders understand exactly what the product would and wouldn't do in their specific setting. Several prospects actually used that model card to decide whether our solution was appropriate for their patient population. One large system in Texas chose not to implement our scheduling module because the documentation made clear our model had known limitations with their rural clinic population. That's the system working as intended.<br><br>I understand the argument for streamlining requirements. Faster market entry, lower costs for developers, more competition. These are not trivial concerns. But in healthcare AI, where algorithms influence decisions about patient care—even something as seemingly simple as appointment scheduling can affect access to care—transparency requirements aren't bureaucratic overhead. They're a mechanism for accountability. When a clinician or administrator is evaluating whether to deploy an AI system in their workflow, they need to know what it was built on, what it's been tested against, and what the known limitations are. Without that information, they're not making an informed decision. They're just hoping the vendor is trustworthy.<br><br>What worries me about the proposed changes is that they appear to create a two-tier system. Companies that use certain API standards would face lighter certification requirements, while vendors using other technical approaches would retain the full burden. That's not actually deregulation—that's regulatory arbitrage. It's a way of saying that the burden doesn't go down; it just shifts. And it creates a perverse incentive for vendors to choose their technical architecture based on which pathway requires less disclosure, rather than based on what actually works best for interoperability and patient safety.<br><br>We're also concerned about what happens when a less-scrutinized AI system gets deployed and fails in ways that weren't documented because the vendor wasn't required to do the analysis. Who bears that risk? The health system that deployed it bears the risk. The patients affected by incorrect clinical decisions bear the risk. The vendor that cut corners on validation to get to market faster? They're already profitable by then.<br><br>I don't think this is an argument for keeping every existing requirement exactly as written. There may be inefficiencies in the current process. There may be ways to streamline documentation without sacrificing substance. But the specific requirement for comprehensive AI model card disclosure is doing essential work. It's making vendors think carefully about whether our algorithms♔♔2026-01-16T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-16T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔2♔♔♔♔TRUE♔3♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔Hospital and health system procurement teams rely on DSI transparency requirements to evaluate whether AI tools are fit for their specific patient populations, clinical workflows, and risk tolerances; removal strips away decision-making data.♔industry♔medium♔frustrated♔Texas♔health IT director♔40♔Last year, we spent eighteen months and nearly $400K getting our patient scheduling module through certification just so it could talk to the big EHR systems—and now I'm hearing they might let competitors skip half those requirements if they use a different API standard. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0018♔CMS-2025-0050♔Daniel Miller♔TMF Health Quality Institute♔♔♔♔TMF Health Quality Institute opposes deregulatory actions that would eliminate AI model card disclosure requirements, arguing that the absence of published outcome studies does not prove these transparency mechanisms lack value, and that removing ...♔RE: Request for Information – Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability: ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions<br><br>TMF Health Quality Institute appreciates the opportunity to respond to this RFI, particularly given our work supporting quality measurement and health data integrity across provider networks. Her cardiologist's records were incomplete when she finally obtained them. She had already moved to a different provider and ended up repeating diagnostic tests. What they're asking for—and what AI model card requirements are meant to provide—is not just access but understanding. When a clinical algorithm influences a treatment recommendation, patients and their doctors deserve to know how that algorithm was trained, what its performance characteristics are, and what assumptions it carries.<br><br>The RFI suggests that detailed disclosures of AI model characteristics impose compliance burden without demonstrated clinical benefit. But here's what concerns us: these transparency requirements are genuinely new. We do not yet have longitudinal outcome data because the field itself is still ramping up. The absence of published studies does not prove ineffectiveness; it reflects that we are living through the early adoption phase. To remove these disclosure requirements now—before we have real-world evidence of their impact—would be like dismantling safety railings while the building is still under construction because we don't yet have a published study proving that railings save lives.<br><br>We urge the agencies to preserve AI model card transparency as a certification requirement, particularly for algorithms that inform clinical decisions. If compliance costs are the concern, we support streamlined submission formats or tiered disclosure requirements based on algorithm risk. But removing the requirement itself creates a gap in accountability at exactly the wrong moment in this technology's maturity curve.<br><br>We are happy to discuss further.<br><br>Respectfully,<br><br>Daniel Miller<br>Executive Director<br>TMF Health Quality Institute<br>Seattle, WA♔♔2025-12-25T00:00:00Z♔2025-12-25T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔1♔♔♔♔TRUE♔1♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔The agency's claim that no public evidence supports transparency's positive impact conflates lack of published studies with lack of real-world benefit; transparency requirements are too recent and nascent to have generated robust outcome literature.♔advocacy_group♔medium♔concerned♔Washington♔executive director♔36♔Last month, a patient called our office in tears because her cardiologist's records—including her entire surgical history—were stuck behind a wall at the hospital system across town; when she finally got copies after six weeks of back-and-forth, the data was incomplete and she had to repeat tests her new doctor had already ordered. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0021♔CMS-2025-0050♔Kenneth Ramirez♔NORC at the University of Chicago♔♔♔♔Loosening EHR certification and transparency requirements before establishing a research infrastructure to measure their effects creates a self-fulfilling prophecy that prevents the generation of evidence needed to evaluate whether cost savings co...♔Last spring, my team at NORC was deep into a longitudinal study tracking EHR adoption across rural Michigan clinics—six sites, three years of data collection planned. AI governance literature in healthcare is barely five years old. Of course we don't have robust longitudinal evidence on model card transparency yet. That lag between policy implementation and measurable outcomes is not a flaw in the evidence base; it's inherent to how medical research actually works.<br><br>What concerns me is the self-fulfilling prophecy this creates. If you remove detailed transparency requirements before we have the ability to study their effects, you eliminate the research infrastructure needed to generate the very comparative effectiveness data the agency says doesn't exist. Researchers can't harmonize datasets across systems with different disclosure standards. We can't track whether cheaper uncertified systems correlate with worse outcomes because we don't have the metadata to conduct that analysis.<br><br>I'm not arguing against innovation or cost reduction. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0023♔CMS-2025-0050♔Margaret Davis♔XSELL Technologies♔♔♔♔XSELL Technologies opposes lowering AI transparency requirements in health IT certification, arguing that removing proactive disclosure mandates prioritizes innovation over patient safety and creates unfair competitive advantage for new entrants w...♔Thank you for the opportunity to comment on the proposed revisions to health IT certification criteria. They're the difference between a clinician understanding what their tool might miss and discovering it through a preventable medical error. Don't create a regulatory class system where my compliance obligation disappears for the startup launching next month. In safety-net settings, we already face adoption friction because vendors have burned us before. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0032♔CMS-2025-0050♔Anthony Allen♔Digital Medicine Society (DiMe)♔♔♔♔While supporting regulatory streamlining for health IT, the commenter urges agencies to retain detailed AI transparency requirements (model cards) for clinical decision-support systems, arguing that clinicians need clear documentation of a tool's ...♔RE: Request for Information Related to Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability: ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions<br><br>Thank you for the opportunity to comment on the proposed streamlining of certification requirements. The Digital Medicine Society appreciates the Administration's commitment to reducing unnecessary regulatory friction in health IT development.<br><br>My perspective on this issue is shaped partly by professional experience and partly by something more immediate. My mother was diagnosed with early-stage Alzheimer's last year. Her neurologist practices in Minneapolis; her primary care physician is in Sioux Falls, where she lives. Over the past twelve months, we've encountered exactly the kind of fragmentation these rules are meant to address. Getting her records transferred between EHR systems has taken months. Her cognitive decline doesn't pause for administrative delays, and neither does the window for early interventions that might slow her disease progression. That's what makes this conversation real for me.<br><br>I want to be clear: I understand the innovation argument for streamlining certification. Developers face compliance costs, and those costs can slow deployment of tools that could help people. But I'd urge the agencies not to remove the detailed AI transparency requirements—the model cards that specify intended use, target population, known limitations, and training data characteristics. Here's why this matters concretely.<br><br>When my mother's care teams need to evaluate an AI-assisted diagnostic tool or a clinical decision support system, they need to know what that system was designed to do. An algorithm trained primarily on younger patients with different symptom presentations may fail silently on an 79-year-old with atypical disease progression. A tool validated on data from academic medical centers may not perform the same way in a smaller rural clinic. Her neurologist in Minneapolis and her primary care doctor in Sioux Falls can't coordinate her care safely if they can't understand how the systems they're relying on actually work.<br><br>Transparency and interoperability aren't competing priorities. They're complementary. Removing certification criteria for detailed AI disclosures creates information asymmetries that leave clinicians flying blind. The very interoperability standards we're fighting for—seamless data exchange across fragmented systems—only work if clinicians can trust what they're seeing and understand how AI is processing that data.<br><br>I'd suggest a middle path: keep the model card requirements for AI systems used in clinical decision-making, particularly those that inform diagnosis or treatment selection for vulnerable populations. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0040♔CMS-2025-0050♔Lisa Adams♔iPill inc♔♔♔♔A healthcare technology vendor warns that removing AI transparency and model card disclosure requirements will trigger a "race to the bottom" where unvalidated systems undercut responsible competitors, ultimately locking clinics into black-box too...♔Re: Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability: ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions To Unleash Prosperity<br><br>My name is Lisa Adams, and I'm the practice manager at iPill inc in Missouri. I couldn't give her a good answer without walking her through technical validation details she didn't care about. That's a problem. We lost the contract.<br><br>What worries me more is that this will accelerate. If you remove model card disclosure requirements and loosen validation criteria before AI adoption becomes standard across clinic networks, you'll create a race to the bottom that responsible vendors can't win. My competitor is faster and cheaper because they're shipping unvalidated systems. Their clinic clients have no way to audit what those systems actually do. The clinic doesn't even know what questions to ask.<br><br>I understand the impulse to reduce certification burden. But removing transparency requirements before widespread AI adoption is the wrong time to do it. Once bad practices become industry standard, they're impossible to unwind. Clinics will be locked into systems they can't inspect or trust. And vendors who maintain rigorous validation will get priced out. That's not innovation. That's a compliance collapse.<br><br>On your specific questions about which criteria to revise: don't touch AI transparency and model card requirements. Those aren't bureaucratic overhead. They're the only mechanism clinics have to distinguish between validated and unvalidated systems. Everything else can be streamlined. But pull transparency standards now, and in three years you'll have a market full of black-box AI tools running in primary care with no audit trail and no way for clinics to know if they're actually working.<br><br>The irony is that strong transparency standards protect responsible vendors too. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0041♔CMS-2025-0050♔Michelle Hall♔SAFI Clinical Informatics Group LLC♔♔♔♔SAFI Clinical Informatics Group argues against removing AI transparency requirements from health IT certification standards, warning that eliminating disclosure mandates for AI decision-support tools before documented failures occur will create a ...♔SAFI Clinical Informatics Group LLC submits this comment on the proposed modifications to health IT certification and information blocking requirements.<br><br>Our organization has spent the last eighteen months and nearly $2 million ensuring our latest EHR module meets current certification standards. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0044♔CMS-2025-0050♔Daniel Nguyen♔The Society of Thoracic Surgeons♔♔♔♔The Society of Thoracic Surgeons opposes removing model card disclosure requirements for AI systems in clinical settings, arguing that transparency standards are essential for accountability and patient safety rather than unnecessary regulatory bu...♔The Society of Thoracic Surgeons appreciates the opportunity to comment on this RFI. What followed was instructive and deeply troubling. When we tried to hold the vendor accountable—to understand what their system had failed to do and why—we found ourselves navigating overlapping regulations with conflicting requirements. The vendor's response was to claim that tightening security would reduce interoperability. The irony was painful: we were told that the only path forward was to accept less transparency about how their systems work, not more.<br><br>That experience shapes how we read the proposal to remove model card disclosure requirements under the DSI certification criterion. On the surface, I understand the deregulatory impulse. Nobody in my position wants unnecessary compliance burden. But I want to be direct: removing transparency requirements for AI and algorithmic systems used in high-stakes clinical decisions is not burden reduction. It's accountability abdication, and it will cost patients and advocacy organizations far more than it saves vendors.<br><br>Here's what concerns me most. When an algorithmic system makes a poor recommendation—when it misses a contraindication, when it systematically underperforms for a particular patient population—clinicians and patients have no documented baseline for understanding why. Was this a limitation the vendor knew about but didn't disclose? A training data bias? A validation gap? Without model cards and transparent disclosure of system limitations, you've shifted the entire burden of discovery onto the people harmed by the failure. That's not innovation. That's privatizing benefit and socializing cost.<br><br>The tension I face is real. I want interoperability. I want clinicians to access patient data across systems. I want competition and innovation in health IT. But I've learned that you don't achieve patient safety by removing the mechanisms that force vendors to be honest about what their systems can and cannot do. Model card disclosures under DSI certification aren't obstacles to innovation—they're the baseline for informed consent.<br><br>Regarding the specific RFI question about which certification criteria should be removed or revised: I would urge extreme caution with any revision that weakens transparency standards around algorithmic systems. The DSI model card requirement is not one of those criteria. If anything, it should expand as AI use in clinical decision support grows. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0050♔CMS-2025-0050♔Daniel Anderson♔Ainsley Advisory Group♔♔♔♔Ainsley Advisory Group supports streamlining compliance processes through safe harbors for established standards and modernizing recertification cycles, but warns against removing transparency and model documentation requirements that enable small...♔Ainsley Advisory Group appreciates the opportunity to comment on this proposed deregulatory framework. They're the mechanism that lets smaller developers compete on merit rather than just capital reserves, and they're what patients and clinicians actually need to make informed decisions about adopting new systems. When a smaller vendor can't afford to document how their algorithm behaves across different patient populations, or what its failure modes look like, they're not just facing a compliance burden—they're locked out of the conversation before they start.<br><br>On certification criteria specifically, we'd suggest examining whether existing interoperability standards like FHIR and SMART App Launch could serve as safe harbors, reducing the need for redundant testing where those standards are properly implemented. That's different from removing transparency requirements wholesale. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0061♔CMS-2025-0050♔Barbara Green♔Dental Standards Institute♔♔♔♔The Dental Standards Institute urges retention of AI model card disclosure requirements in the proposed deregulatory framework, arguing that while some streamlining of compliance processes is appropriate, eliminating transparency mechanisms for AI...♔The Dental Standards Institute appreciates the opportunity to comment on the proposed deregulatory framework under the Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability initiative. Our organization has spent the better part of two decades working with dental practices, health systems, and technology vendors to establish meaningful standards for clinical data management and AI integration in oral healthcare. We represent over 180 member organizations across Minnesota, Wisconsin, and the broader Upper Midwest, and our policy work focuses specifically on how regulatory frameworks either enable or impede the safe adoption of health information technology in dental settings.<br><br>I want to be direct about why we are submitting this comment: last year, one of our member clinics in Duluth experienced a data breach that crystallized for us what happens when transparency requirements are weakened in pursuit of deregulation. A vendor with access to patient records—access granted through what appeared to be routine business associate arrangements—exploited a gap in the clinic's data governance system and accessed complete treatment histories, including sensitive notes about a patient's substance abuse recovery. The breach notification process worked as intended, technically speaking. What didn't work was the accountability mechanism. The vendor faced no meaningful enforcement action, and because the data had been "anonymized," there was no clear legal basis to prevent them from selling derivative datasets to competing practices. This experience has shaped how we think about what deregulation actually means in practice.<br><br>The proposed rule raises genuine concerns about how far the pendulum should swing in reducing certification requirements, particularly those relating to AI model transparency. We want to be careful here—we are not arguing against reducing burden on developers or streamlining compliance processes where that makes sense. The dental IT market is smaller and more fragmented than general healthcare IT, and we recognize that overly prescriptive standards can slow innovation and create barriers for smaller vendors. But we also think there is a significant difference between reducing administrative burden and removing transparency mechanisms that serve patient safety and clinical accountability.<br><br>Specifically, we urge the agency to retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures. This criterion requires vendors to document intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes before certification. We know this adds documentation work. We also know it is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in healthcare AI applications, and we think the deregulatory impulse has not adequately reckoned with what gets lost when these requirements disappear.<br><br>Here is why this matters concretely. A dental practice implementing an AI-assisted diagnostic tool needs to know whether that tool was trained on imaging data that included diverse patient populations or whether it was predominantly trained on scans from patients with particular demographic characteristics. This is not a technical nicety. The literature on algorithmic bias in medical imaging is substantial, and dentistry is not immune to these issues. When a practice adopts a tool without understanding its training data composition, they are assuming blind risk. The model card requirement gives the practice—and the patient—a basis for understanding what that tool can and cannot reliably do. Without it, the practice is operating on faith in the vendor's assertions.<br><br>The second piece is clinical accountability. When something goes wrong—when a tool produces a false negative and a patient's oral cancer is missed, or when a diagnostic recommendation is systematically biased against certain patient populations—there needs to be a clear mechanism for understanding how the tool was built and validated. The model card documentation creates that mechanism. It is not a perfect safeguard, but it is the baseline transparency required for meaningful accountability. A practice cannot investigate what went wrong if the vendor never disclosed what the tool was designed to do, what data it was trained on, or what its known limitations are.<br><br>We have also considered the vendor perspective, and we think some of the concerns about burden are legitimate. Smaller companies, in particular, may struggle with the administrative cost of producing comprehensive model card documentation. But the solution to this problem is not to eliminate the requirement—it is to streamline the process, provide clearer templates, or consider scaled requirements based on vendor size and deployment scope. The FDA has done this in other contexts. The requirement itself is not the burden; the vagueness of the requirement is. Clear, standardized documentation is actually easier to produce than ambiguous, ad hoc disclosures.<br><br>What also concerns us is the broader trajectory. The proposed rule frames certification requirements as obstacles to innovation and market entry. In some cases, this framing is fair. But certification requirements exist because healthcare is not a normal market. Patients cannot easily switch providers to punish bad vendors. The information asymmetry between vendors and clinical users is profound. Network effects lock practices into particular vendors and their tools. In this context, upfront transparency requirements are not anti-competitive obstacles—they are the market mechanism that allows clinicians to make informed choices. Removing them does not unleash competition; it removes the information♔♔2026-01-02T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-02T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔2♔♔♔♔TRUE♔3♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔The 'black box' nature of unvetted AI algorithms represents a systemic adoption barrier; model cards are one of the few existing standards that demystify AI design and build the trust necessary for responsible clinical deployment.♔advocacy_group♔high♔frustrated♔Minnesota♔policy director♔59♔Last year, one of our member clinics in Duluth discovered that a patient's complete treatment history—including notes about substance abuse recovery—had been accessed by an unauthorized vendor through a gap in their records system, and while the breach notification requirements kicked in, there was genuinely no clear enforcement mechanism to hold the third party accountable or prevent them from selling anonymized derivatives of that data to competitors.♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔♔♔True♔♔829

CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0064♔CMS-2025-0050♔William Martin♔CareQuest Institute for Oral Health♔♔♔♔Deregulation efforts that eliminate transparency requirements like Developer System Information disclosures shift due diligence burdens onto health systems rather than reducing overall burden, and faster data exchange can be achieved without gutti...♔William Martin, Policy Director<br>CareQuest Institute for Oral Health<br>Austin, Texas<br><br>RE: Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability: ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions To Unleash Prosperity (RFI)<br><br>The tension I encounter most often in our work isn't between innovation and regulation—it's between two legitimate but contradictory pressures: the need for faster data exchange and the responsibility to understand what tools we're actually deploying in clinical settings. Last year, we lost a patient to periodontal disease progression that might have been preventable if she could have moved her records between practices within weeks instead of months. The system locked her data for liability reasons. But when I brought the problem to our board, our compliance officers pointed out that loosening those restrictions without transparency about *how* the data would be used and *what algorithms* would interpret it could create different liabilities—ones we couldn't quantify because we had no visibility into vendor systems.<br><br>That real-world bind is what concerns me about the direction of this deregulation effort.<br><br>CareQuest has helped dental and health systems across 20 states evaluate digital health tools. What I've learned is that hospital procurement teams—the people who actually decide whether to deploy an algorithm or interoperability standard—are operating blind when transparency requirements vanish. They don't need less regulation. They need better information to make responsible decisions. When you strip away Developer System Information model card disclosures in the name of reducing burden, you don't eliminate the burden. You shift it onto health systems that now must conduct expensive, parallel due diligence on their own, hire consultants to reverse-engineer vendor tools, or worse, adopt systems they don't fully understand because they lack the leverage to demand answers.<br><br>I'm not opposed to streamlining certification criteria or reducing friction in standards development. Those are separate from transparency. USCDI interoperability standards and DSI disclosure requirements serve different functions. You can make data exchange faster without gutting the disclosure infrastructure that lets clinicians and procurement teams know what they're buying. What worries me is that the RFI conflates those two things.<br><br>The specific question about revision to maintenance of certification requirements: I'd push back on removing reporting requirements tied to actual clinical deployment. Keep the certification thresholds lean, sure. But health systems need model cards that describe *what the tool was trained on*, *what populations it's been tested in*, and *what performance degradation looks like outside those populations*. That's not theater. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0065♔CMS-2025-0050♔Michelle King♔Federation of State Medical Boards♔♔♔♔The Federation of State Medical Boards supports health IT deregulation to improve interoperability but opposes removing the Data Segmentation for Privacy certification requirement for AI model transparency, arguing that physicians need detailed in...♔March 15, 2025<br><br>Office of the National Coordinator for Health Information Technology<br>U.S. Department of Health and Human Services<br>330 C Street SW<br>Washington, DC 20201<br><br>Re: Public Comment on Health Data, Technology, and Interoperability: ASTP/ONC Deregulatory Actions<br><br>Dear HHS Leadership:<br><br>The Federation of State Medical Boards writes to express our strong support for efforts to streamline health information exchange and reduce unnecessary regulatory burden on health IT developers and providers. We recognize the Office of the National Coordinator's commitment to enabling innovation and competition in the health technology market, and we share the goal of making it easier for patients and providers to access and move health information across systems. That said, we have a specific concern about one proposed deregulatory action that we believe warrants careful reconsideration: the removal of the Data Segmentation for Privacy (DSI) certification criterion that currently requires developers of health IT systems to disclose detailed information about artificial intelligence models used in clinical decision support and other high-stakes healthcare applications.<br><br>Before explaining our position, I should note that this concern arose from a real situation that affected one of our board members' patients. Last year, an elderly woman switched cardiologists after moving closer to her family. The previous practice could not quickly transfer her records in a usable format—they claimed the systems simply wouldn't export the data in a way the new practice could import. The new cardiologist had to order three months of diagnostic testing from scratch. The patient paid thousands out of pocket, and her treatment plan was delayed by weeks. Everyone involved was frustrated, and frankly, angry. When we learned that the proposed rules might make record transfers faster and easier, we thought finally, something is going to fix this problem. But then we started reading more carefully about what deregulation might mean, and we realized that speed without security, and access without accountability, could create different kinds of harm.<br><br>This brings us to the AI model card requirement under the DSI criterion. We understand the appeal of removing this requirement. Developers have told us it adds cost and complexity. The certification process takes time. There are legitimate concerns about burden. But we believe the current requirement serves a critical function that shouldn't be discarded lightly, and we want to explain why from the perspective of state medical boards who oversee physician practice.<br><br>**Why AI Transparency Matters in Healthcare**<br><br>State medical boards regulate physicians and are responsible for protecting the public from incompetent or unsafe practice. When a physician uses a health IT system that incorporates AI—whether it's a diagnostic support tool, a clinical workflow recommendation system, or a predictive analytics application—the physician is responsible for understanding how that tool works and whether it's appropriate for their patient. But here's the problem: most physicians cannot independently verify whether an AI model is safe, accurate, or suitable for their specific patient population. They lack the technical expertise, and they don't have access to the training data, validation studies, or known limitations of the models.<br><br>This creates a transparency gap. A cardiologist in Massachusetts cannot know whether a diagnostic recommendation system was trained on data that adequately represents elderly women with complex comorbidities, or whether the model performs equally well across different racial and ethnic groups, or whether it has known failure modes in certain clinical contexts. Without that information, the physician is essentially using a black box. They may believe they're practicing evidence-based medicine when they're actually relying on a system whose performance characteristics are unknown to them.<br><br>The current DSI certification criterion requires developers to disclose, in plain language, the intended use of AI models, the target population for which they were designed, known risks and limitations, characteristics of the training data, and the validation processes used. This isn't an onerous requirement. It's asking developers to write down what they should already know about their own products. And it's asking them to make that information available to the physicians and healthcare systems that use those products.<br><br>When we talk to physicians about this, they tell us they want this information. They don't want to be surprised by limitations that could affect patient care. They want to know if a model was validated in a diverse population or only in a narrow subset. They want to know if there are known accuracy problems in certain scenarios. They want to understand the reasoning behind the system's recommendations well enough to explain it to patients. These aren't unreasonable asks.<br><br>**The Connection to Patient Safety and Clinical Adoption**<br><br>There's also a practical question of clinical adoption. Right now, many physicians are skeptical of AI-based clinical decision support tools. Some of that skepticism is warranted—there have been well-publicized failures and biases in healthcare AI systems. But some of it is simply uncertainty. Physicians don't know whether to trust a system because they can't see inside it. If we♔♔2025-12-09T00:00:00Z♔2025-12-09T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔2♔♔♔♔TRUE♔3♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔The agency's claim that no public evidence supports transparency's positive impact conflates lack of published studies with lack of real-world benefit; transparency requirements are too recent and nascent to have generated robust outcome literature.♔advocacy_group♔medium♔frustrated♔Massachusetts♔research director♔47♔Last year, one of our board members' elderly patients switched cardiologists and had to repeat three months of diagnostic testing because the previous practice claimed they couldn't quickly transfer the records in a usable format—the patient's new doctor literally had to start from scratch. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0066♔CMS-2025-0050♔Dorothy Young♔NORC at the University of Chicago♔♔♔♔Removing AI model card disclosure and certification requirements would recreate vendor lock-in problems by eliminating transparency mechanisms that allow clinicians and smaller health systems to assess algorithmic bias and performance, ultimately ...♔Over the past year, I've analyzed claims data from three major health systems in the tri-state area looking at care coordination for patients with complex chronic conditions. What I found was systematic delays in treatment initiation and medication reconciliation—not because the technology didn't exist, but because the vendors' electronic systems were designed not to talk to each other. Interoperability would have been economically rational from a clinical standpoint. From a business standpoint, it threatened their market position. That's the vendor lock-in problem that certification standards were meant to address.<br><br>A colleague mentioned his mother had a medication interaction between her cardiologist and rheumatologist that might have been caught if their records had actually synchronized. She's one of thousands. This isn't abstract for me anymore.<br><br>I want to be direct about what concerns me with the current proposal. The request to remove AI model card disclosure requirements and revise certification criteria sounds efficiency-minded in principle. In practice, it eliminates the empirical foundation that health economists, clinicians, and smaller health systems need to assess whether algorithmic tools are actually reducing costs and improving outcomes, or just shifting risk to vulnerable populations. Without transparency requirements, vendors have no incentive to disclose algorithmic bias, cost effectiveness data, or population-specific performance metrics. Smaller safety-net providers and community health systems can't conduct due diligence on vendor claims. We end up back where we started—with information asymmetries that favor large vendors and lock out competition.<br><br>I've read the economic case for deregulation. It assumes perfect information and competitive markets. Health IT markets are neither. The certification criteria for AI-based decision support tools function as a transparency mechanism that levels an uneven playing field. Remove them, and information rents increase for incumbents. Clinicians lose the ability to detect systematic algorithmic bias before it scales across populations.<br><br>I'm not arguing against innovation. I'm arguing that transparency is pro-competition. Model card disclosures don't slow down good vendors. They slow down bad ones. If you want to reduce burden on developers, I'd suggest narrowing the scope of required disclosures rather than eliminating them—specify which performance metrics matter most for different use cases. But don't remove the requirement to disclose. That repeats the mistake we made with interoperability standards in the first place.♔♔2025-12-11T00:00:00Z♔2025-12-11T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔1♔♔♔♔TRUE♔2♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔Removing transparency requirements for AI-based DSIs directly undermines patient safety by eliminating clinician access to critical performance and risk data needed to detect and prevent algorithmic harm.♔academic♔high♔concerned♔New Jersey♔health economist♔52♔Last year, I analyzed claims data from three major health systems in the tri-state area and discovered that patients with complex chronic conditions were experiencing systematic delays in care coordination because their providers' electronic systems couldn't communicate—not for technical reasons, but because vendor lock-in made interoperability economically irrational for smaller practices. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0078♔CMS-2025-0050♔George Green♔MCG Health, part of the Hearst Health network♔♔♔♔A health system policy analyst argues that removing AI model card disclosure requirements from certification criteria would be a mistake, as vendors' refusal to disclose training data, validation processes, and known failure modes prevents health ...♔To the Office of the National Coordinator for Health Information Technology:<br><br>I work as a senior policy analyst at MCG Health, part of the Hearst Health network in Massachusetts. We're a mid-sized health system with multiple hospital campuses and primary care practices, so we interact with health IT vendors constantly—both the large EHR vendors and smaller third-party developers trying to build tools for patient engagement and care coordination. What I'm going to say here comes from actually trying to make these systems work in practice, not from some abstract policy perspective.<br><br>Last month we discovered that our primary EHR vendor had been throttling API requests from a patient's third-party health app without our knowledge. They claimed it was a "security concern," but when we dug into it, it became clear they were using API throttling as contractual leverage to push us toward their own competing apps. When we tried to audit the actual data flows—to verify that patient information was being properly protected during transfer—we hit a wall. The vendor's APIs are essentially black boxes. We couldn't see the training data characteristics, the validation processes, the known failure modes, anything that would let us assess whether the system was actually safe. And we're the paying customer.<br><br>That experience made me pay close attention to the proposal to remove the DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures. I need to be direct: I think that would be a mistake, and I want to explain why from the perspective of someone actually trying to implement this technology in a health system.<br><br>The current certification requirement asks vendors to disclose intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes. This sounds like it's about transparency for transparency's sake. But it's not. It's about accountability in systems that directly affect patient safety. And I've learned that the hard way.<br><br>Here's the reality: when a vendor refuses to disclose how their AI model was trained, what populations it was validated on, or what its known failure modes are, you can't make an informed decision about whether to use it. You can't audit it. You can't know whether it's safe for your patient population. At MCG Health, we serve a lot of patients with complex comorbidities—our patient population skews older than national averages, with higher rates of chronic kidney disease and diabetes. If a vendor's AI model was trained primarily on younger, healthier populations, we need to know that. We need to know the model's performance degraded when applied to patients with certain characteristics. Without that information, we're essentially flying blind.<br><br>The argument I'm hearing from the deregulation side is that certification requirements create burden on vendors, slow down innovation, and that market competition will naturally weed out unsafe products. I understand that logic. And I'm not opposed to reducing unnecessary regulatory burden. But there's a category difference between a requirement that's genuinely unnecessary and one that's essential for informed decision-making. Model card disclosures are the latter.<br><br>When I look at what happened with our EHR vendor and the API throttling, the underlying problem wasn't that there was too much regulation. The problem was that we had no visibility into what they were doing. We couldn't verify claims about security. We couldn't compare their system to alternatives because we couldn't actually see inside their systems. And because we're locked into a multiyear contract with significant switching costs, we had very little leverage to demand better. In that context, certification criteria that require transparency aren't about creating burden—they're about creating a level playing field where health systems can actually make informed choices.<br><br>I also want to push back on the assumption that market competition alone will solve this. Healthcare technology markets aren't perfectly competitive. There are high switching costs. There's significant vendor lock-in. And there's information asymmetry—vendors know far more about their products than purchasers do. In that environment, certification criteria that mandate transparency aren't anti-market. They're pro-market. They level the information playing field so that health systems can actually make the choices that the theory of competitive markets assumes we can make.<br><br>There's another piece of this that matters for patient safety. When we implement an AI system in a clinical workflow, we need to understand its failure modes. We need to know: what happens when the model encounters data it wasn't trained on? What's the performance on edge cases? What are the documented differences in accuracy across demographic groups? These aren't academic questions. They're practical questions that affect patient care. If a vendor won't disclose this information, we have to assume either they don't know it—which is a problem—or they know it and don't want us to know—which is also a problem. Either way, requiring model card disclosures forces vendors to actually understand their own systems and be honest about what they do and don't do well.♔♔2025-12-10T00:00:00Z♔2025-12-10T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔2♔♔♔♔TRUE♔2♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔Removing transparency requirements for AI-based DSIs directly undermines patient safety by eliminating clinician access to critical performance and risk data needed to detect and prevent algorithmic harm.♔advocacy_group♔high♔angry♔Massachusetts♔senior policy analyst♔32♔Last month, I discovered that our EHR vendor had been throttling API requests from a patient's third-party health app without our knowledge, citing "security concerns" that turned out to be contractual leverage—and when we tried to audit the actual data flows, we hit a wall of proprietary obfuscation that made it impossible to verify whether patient information was even being properly protected during transfer.♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔♔♔True♔♔807
CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0079♔CMS-2025-0050♔Ashley Ramirez♔♔♔♔♔The commenter supports improved medical record accessibility but opposes weakening transparency and certification standards for AI systems and data handling, arguing that the lack of current evidence for these protections does not justify removing...♔My daughter's experience last year is exactly why I'm worried about this. That doesn't make sense to me. These rules are brand new. Of course there aren't studies showing they work yet—nobody's had time to measure it. That's when patients get wrong diagnoses. And when companies don't have to meet certain standards for handling data, that's when people's private medical information gets at risk. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0082♔CMS-2025-0050♔Charles Thomas♔Regard Technologies, Inc.♔♔♔♔A healthcare IT vendor argues for maintaining detailed model card requirements in AI certification standards, contending that transparency about tool limitations and training data is essential for clinician understanding and patient safety, even t...♔Thank you for the opportunity to comment on these proposed changes to health IT certification standards.
