We documented everything—intended use cases, target populations, known limitations, training data characteristics, validation methodology. It was thorough and expensive. Then we watched a competitor launch something functionally similar in six weeks, cutting corners we thought were mandatory. When that happens, you start questioning whether you did the right thing.<br><br>But here's what I've learned in healthcare IT: the temptation to lighten the load always feels good until a patient gets harmed. Model card transparency isn't bureaucratic theater. It's the difference between clinicians understanding what a tool can and cannot do, and clinicians trusting something they don't actually understand. In high-stakes healthcare applications, that gap kills people.<br><br>The model card requirement—detailed disclosure of intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes—serves a function that's hard to see until it's missing. When a hospital implements an AI-assisted diagnostic tool, their clinical leadership needs to know what populations that tool was validated on. If it was trained primarily on data from academic medical centers and they're deploying it in a rural critical access hospital, they need to see that mismatch in writing. They need the model card to say it. Without it, they're flying blind.<br><br>I understand the argument about faster time-to-market and lower compliance costs. Believe me, I do. But certification standards exist because the alternative—the Wild West approach where vendors self-certify or claim their tools are "general purpose" and therefore unregulated—has a track record. We've seen it in other industries. And we're seeing early warning signs in healthcare AI right now.<br><br>What troubles me is that if we remove this requirement, we're not actually removing burden equally. Large vendors like us have compliance infrastructure and legal teams. We can absorb regulatory uncertainty. Smaller developers might compete on price by cutting corners on transparency. Hospitals might choose based on cost, not on how well they actually understand what they're buying. That's not innovation—that's a race to the bottom.<br><br>The eighteen months and $400,000 we spent? I'm not asking for pity. But I am saying it created something valuable: it forced us to truly understand our own tool's limitations. That exercise made us better. The model card we produced isn't just a checkbox for regulators. Our sales team uses it. Our support team uses it. Customers ask for it. Removing the requirement doesn't remove the need—it just removes the assurance that everyone's doing it.<br><br>I'd propose a middle path. Keep the DSI model card requirement for any AI-assisted clinical decision support or diagnostic tools. Consider streamlining it for lower-risk applications like scheduling or billing. But for anything that touches patient care decisions, the transparency has to stay. Certification should mean something. It should mean a clinician can read a document and know what they're implementing.<br><br>We'll comply with whatever standards you set. But please don't set them lower because we're tired of compliance. We're tired of compliance partly because we do it right.♔♔2025-12-12T00:00:00Z♔2025-12-12T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔1♔♔♔♔TRUE♔3♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔The agency's claim that no public evidence supports transparency's positive impact conflates lack of published studies with lack of real-world benefit; transparency requirements are too recent and nascent to have generated robust outcome literature.♔industry♔medium♔angry♔Missouri♔healthcare administrator♔54♔Last year, we spent eighteen months and nearly $400,000 getting our patient scheduling module certified under the old standards, only to watch a competitor launch something similar in six weeks without jumping through half the hoops. Robust longitudinal studies don't materialize overnight. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0088♔CMS-2025-0050♔Deborah Thompson♔Verve, LLC♔♔♔♔Proactive AI transparency requirements (model card disclosure) in DSI certification would create a level playing field for startups and smaller vendors by establishing one clear baseline standard upfront, whereas waiting for documented harms befor...♔Deborah Thompson<br>Health IT Director<br>Verve, LLC<br>Kentucky<br><br>Last year, we were seriously considering acquiring a health IT startup that had built something genuinely useful—a clinical decision support tool that could have made a real difference for our providers. Model cards are functionally similar to interoperability standards—they reduce information asymmetries that can lead to clinical errors and inefficient care. The difference is timing.<br><br>These certification criteria for AI transparency are new. Really new. Robust outcome literature doesn't materialize in months. It takes years of real-world implementation, careful measurement, and peer review. It should have been straightforward—we're talking about basic demographic data, procedure codes, and patient outcomes. But the notice requests comment on removing detailed AI model card disclosures—the requirement that developers disclose intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes. On that specific point, I want to push back, and I want to do it from the perspective of someone who has sat in both seats: as a health economist trying to use health IT infrastructure for research, and as a clinician trying to deploy tools responsibly in a high-stakes environment.<br><br>The model card requirement isn't regulatory friction. It's foundational transparency.<br><br>Here's what I mean. Last year, we evaluated three different AI-based risk stratification tools for our atrial fibrillation screening program. All three were marketed as validated, all three had FDA clearance or were under review, and all three came with very different performance profiles depending on patient demographics. One tool had been trained predominantly on data from patients over 65. Another was built on a cohort that was 78 percent male. A third had been validated in academic medical centers but we had no information about how it would perform in our safety-net clinic population, where comorbidity burden and medication adherence look different. Without the model card requirement—without being forced to disclose those training characteristics, those known limitations, that specificity about target populations—we would have had to reverse-engineer that information ourselves through pilot testing. We did pilot testing anyway, but only because we're large enough and skeptical enough to invest the time.<br><br>Most institutions aren't. Most will deploy a tool, assume it works for everyone, and find out six months later that it's missing diagnoses in specific subgroups. That's not innovation. That's risk transfer. And the cost of that risk transfer shows up not in vendor balance sheets but in patient outcomes and in the liability exposure of hospitals that deployed tools without understanding their limitations.<br><br>I want to be clear about what I'm not saying. I'm not saying the certification process is well-calibrated. I'm not saying we should require model cards for every minor software update or every incremental improvement to an existing tool. I'm not saying the current ONC Health IT Certification Program is the gold standard. It has real problems. Vendors do spend inordinate amounts of time and money on compliance that doesn't meaningfully improve patient safety. But the solution to bad regulation isn't no regulation; it's better regulation, more narrowly tailored to the actual risks.<br><br>The distinction matters because AI in healthcare is genuinely different from traditional software. A charting system has poor UX and you get frustrated. A diagnostic AI tool trained on a dataset that doesn't represent your patient population and you miss diagnoses in vulnerable groups. Those aren't equivalent problems. The FDA recognized this in its 2021 action plan on AI/ML-based software as a medical device. That recognition was correct.<br><br>What concerns me about the deregulatory proposal is that it collapses this distinction. It treats all regulatory requirements as equivalent friction, without discriminating between requirements that address genuine safety issues and requirements that are genuinely redundant. The model card requirement addresses a safety issue. It's asking vendors to disclose information that clinicians and health systems need in order to deploy tools responsibly. That's not burden; that's baseline transparency.<br><br>Let me give you a concrete example. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0091♔CMS-2025-0050♔Karen Clark♔♔♔♔♔The commenter opposes weakening certification requirements for AI systems in healthcare, arguing that detailed model card disclosures—which document how AI systems work, who they were tested on, and known risks—are essential accountability measure...♔My daughter's pediatrician in Reno switched to a new electronic health system last year, and it was a nightmare. Right now, companies have to provide detailed information about how their AI models work—what they're designed to do, who they're supposed to work for, what the known risks are, what data was used to train them. This transparency requirement exists for a reason. When a pediatrician's office implements a new system with AI running parts of it, I need to know that someone actually checked whether that system was tested on kids like my daughter, whether it's known to have problems with certain populations, whether there are documented risks.<br><br>The certification standards that require these "model cards"—detailed disclosures about AI systems—aren't bureaucratic busywork. They're basic accountability. If something goes wrong, if my daughter's records get mixed up with another patient's, or if an AI system flags something incorrectly, I want to know that the system was properly vetted before it was put into a clinic. Right now, that vetting is part of the certification process.<br><br>I understand the argument that reducing certification requirements will speed up innovation and let smaller companies compete. But there's a difference between streamlining paperwork and removing safeguards. Healthcare isn't like other industries. When a software glitch happens in a health system, it doesn't just inconvenience someone—it can directly affect patient safety. My daughter's wrong vaccination dates are a small example, but they illustrate the point. If I hadn't caught it, her school might have thought she wasn't properly immunized. That's not just an administrative error.<br><br>What I'm asking is that you keep the detailed disclosure requirements for AI systems used in healthcare. These don't have to be burdensome. They just need to exist. Require companies to document what their AI systems are designed to do, who they were tested on, what problems they've encountered, what the limitations are. This gives healthcare providers and patients actual information to work with. It creates accountability.<br><br>I'm not against deregulation across the board. But I think there's a difference between removing unnecessary red tape and removing the transparency measures that actually protect people. The model card requirements are transparent. They're manageable. And they matter when my family's health information is at stake.<br><br>I'm asking that you retain the current certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures for any health IT systems that will be used in clinical settings. Let the system designers innovate on features and functionality. But don't remove the requirement that they explain how their AI systems actually work and what risks they carry. The fact that we haven't yet published a randomized controlled trial proving it helps doesn't make it less foundational.<br><br>If you remove detailed transparency requirements in the name of reducing burden, you've essentially created two markets: one where vendors like us compete on safety and openness, and one where competitors cut corners and still undercut our prices. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0098♔CMS-2025-0050♔Betty Lopez♔beHuman Corp.♔♔♔♔While supporting regulatory streamlining to reduce burdensome documentation and audit requirements that disproportionately harm smaller vendors, the commenter argues that mandatory AI model transparency disclosures should remain non-negotiable saf...♔Thank you for the opportunity to respond to this Request for Information on health IT certification reform. The process nearly destroyed us financially. We hired an outside consultant just to navigate the documentation requirements—that consultant cost more than some of our early employees. I watched competitors with deeper pockets absorb that burden like it was nothing. Smaller vendors either hired consultants or they didn't launch. That's not competition. That's a tax on innovation.<br><br>So I understand the impulse behind this deregulatory action. I do. But I'm also watching my daughter's startup prepare to enter this market, and what keeps me up at night is that we might just shuffle the burden around instead of actually reducing it.<br><br>Here's what concerns me: if we strip away process requirements—the documentation, the audit trails, the certification busywork—that's genuinely helpful. Those things slow down the small players more than the big ones. But there's a line between reducing burden and reducing safety. And I think that line sits exactly where AI transparency needs to be.<br><br>The reason I say this is that AI model disclosures aren't like most certification criteria. They're not paperwork for paperwork's sake. They're prevention. Right now, we're in a window where health AI adoption is accelerating, but we don't yet have documented clinical failures because the systems haven't been in widespread use long enough. Waiting for those failures—waiting to see a patient harmed because a vendor hid the limitations of their algorithm—that's not acceptable. That's ethically indefensible, and it will destroy public trust in healthcare AI faster than anything else.<br><br>But here's the thing that nobody seems to notice: mandatory, clear AI model card disclosure actually levels the playing field for smaller vendors. Right now, the large vendors can afford to obscure complexity. They have teams that can make things sound simpler than they are, and their customers are locked in anyway. A startup like my daughter's? She competes on merit. If the certification barrier is "prove what your model does and doesn't do," then transparency becomes her advantage, not her burden. Smaller teams are often more willing to acknowledge limitations because they don't have corporate legal teams second-guessing every word.<br><br>The certification burden argument shouldn't apply to safety-critical transparency requirements. We can absolutely streamline the process documentation. We can remove redundant audit trails and consolidate submission formats. But the substantive requirement to disclose what AI systems actually do—their training data limitations, their known failure modes, their performance across different patient populations—that needs to stay non-negotiable. It just needs to be standardized so it's not a different burden for every vendor.<br><br>My question for the agency is whether the revision process can distinguish between burden that obscures accountability and burden that streamlines it. Because what I'm worried about is that a one-size-fits-all deregulation will inadvertently protect the vendors who benefit from opacity while making it harder for the innovative smaller players to compete on transparency.<br><br>I'd rather see certification criteria streamlined around AI disclosure than eliminated from it.♔♔2025-12-07T00:00:00Z♔2025-12-07T00:00:00Z♔2025-11-30T00:00:00Z♔2026-01-29T00:00:00Z♔1♔♔♔♔TRUE♔2♔Retain the current DSI certification criterion requiring detailed AI model card disclosures (intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, validation processes) on the grounds that transparency is foundational to patient safety, clinical adoption, and accountability in high-stakes healthcare AI applications.♔Few DSIs are currently AI-based, but the criterion must be in place proactively to prevent harm as AI adoption accelerates; waiting for documented failures before requiring transparency is ethically indefensible.♔industry♔medium♔hopeful♔New Hampshire♔VP of regulatory affairs♔58♔My team spent eighteen months getting our patient engagement platform through the old certification process, and it nearly bankrupted us—we had to hire an outside consultant just to navigate the documentation requirements. CMS-2025-0050-SYNTH-0099♔CMS-2025-0050♔Kevin Campbell♔National Alliance for Care at Home♔♔♔♔The commenter opposes removing AI model card disclosure requirements from health IT certification standards, arguing that transparency about how algorithmic systems work is essential for patient safety, clinical trust, and accountability—and that ...♔The National Alliance for Care at Home appreciates the opportunity to comment on the proposed deregulatory actions outlined in this notice. Our organization has spent the past decade working with patients, family caregivers, and home-based care providers to ensure that health information flows where it matters most—to the people making day-to-day decisions about their own care or their loved ones' care. That mission has taught us something that might seem obvious but apparently isn't: patients need information they can understand and trust, and the systems delivering that information need to be built on a foundation of transparency and genuine security.<br><br>My own experience three years ago with my mother's dementia diagnosis was, frankly, a wake-up call. We couldn't get her records in one place because four different Milwaukee providers said their systems couldn't talk to each other. One clinic asked for a $50 fee to release her digital files. Another told us their "compatibility issues" meant we'd have to request information in person, on paper, every time. But the thing that really scared us—that I still think about—was when one of those providers called us six months after the fact to say they'd had a security incident. They didn't find it themselves. An outside researcher caught it. We never learned exactly what was exposed, and I spent weeks wondering if my mother's cognitive decline was being tracked somewhere she didn't know about, by someone she'd never met.<br><br>That experience is why I'm writing this comment, and why I want to be direct about what concerns me in this proposal. I understand the argument that certification requirements are burdensome. I get that health IT vendors face costs and compliance headaches. But the deregulatory action you're considering—particularly the proposal to remove detailed AI model card disclosures from the Data Segmentation for Privacy (DSI) certification criterion—feels like we're solving the wrong problem at the expense of patient safety.<br><br>Let me be clear about what I think the rule should do. The current DSI certification criterion requires that certified health IT systems disclose detailed model cards for any artificial intelligence or algorithmic decision-making. Those model cards have to include intended use, target population, known risks, training data characteristics, and validation processes. It's dense, technical stuff. It's not something my mother would read. But it's something her doctor should read before using a system to flag medication interactions, predict her hospitalization risk, or decide which services to recommend. Right now, if you're a clinic in Wisconsin or anywhere else, you can't even know what you're putting into the patient record because the vendors haven't disclosed how their AI systems work.<br><br>The proposal to eliminate or water down these disclosures troubles me for reasons that go beyond compliance burden. Three of them stand out.<br><br>First, transparency is the only real protection patients have in a healthcare system where they're fundamentally dependent on their providers' judgment. My mother couldn't evaluate whether her clinic's electronic health record system was giving her doctors good information or biased information. She had no way to know. That asymmetry is baked into the patient-provider relationship, and I'm not sure we can fix it entirely. But we can at least require that the tools making decisions about her care are documented in a way that lets her doctor—and ultimately her—know what's happening. When you remove AI model card requirements, you're not just reducing burden on vendors. You're eliminating the mechanism by which clinicians and patients can understand whether the systems they're relying on are trustworthy. You're making that asymmetry deeper.<br><br>Second, clinical adoption depends on trust. This is something I hear from home-based care providers all the time. They're skeptical of health IT because they've been burned before—they've implemented systems that didn't work, didn't integrate with other systems, or gave them information they couldn't use. When vendors can't or won't explain how their AI systems work, providers assume the worst. They either don't use the systems at all, or they use them while keeping detailed manual notes on the side, which defeats the whole purpose of digitization. Transparency doesn't burden adoption; in a lot of cases, it enables it. Providers want to know what they're using. Patients want to know what's being used on them.<br><br>Third, and maybe most important: accountability means nothing without disclosure. If something goes wrong—if an AI system flags a false medication interaction, or misidentifies a patient's risk category, or systematically performs worse for certain populations—there's no way to trace back to what happened. You can't audit a system you don't understand. You can't identify bias in training data you've never seen. You can't report it. You can't learn from it.
